<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Deobandi" title="Template:Deobandi"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Deobandi" title="Template talk:Deobandi"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>

<p>The <b>Taliban</b> (<a href="/wiki/Pashto_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Pashto language">Pashto</a>: <span lang="ps" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ps"><span class="Nastaliq" title="Nastaliq" style="font-size: 125%; font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq';">طالبان</span></span>‎ <i><span title="Pashto transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">ṭālibān</span></i> "students"), alternatively spelled <b>Taleban</b>, which refers to itself as the <b>Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan (IEA)</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> <a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">Islamic fundamentalist</a> political movement in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> currently waging war (an <a href="/wiki/Insurgency" title="Insurgency">insurgency</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Jihad#Warfare_.28Jihad_bil_Saif.29" title="Jihad">jihad</a>) within that country.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnett_Rubin_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett_Rubin-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Since 2016, the Taliban's leader is Mawlawi <a href="/wiki/Hibatullah_Akhundzada" title="Hibatullah Akhundzada">Hibatullah Akhundzada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Akhundzada_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Akhundzada-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1996 to 2001, the Taliban <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan#Taliban_Emirate_and_Northern_Alliance" title="Afghanistan">held power over roughly three quarters of Afghanistan</a>, and enforced there a strict interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a>, or Islamic law. The Taliban emerged in 1994 as one of the prominent factions in the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">Afghan Civil War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and largely consisted of students recently trained in <a href="/wiki/Madrassas_in_Pakistan" title="Madrassas in Pakistan">madrassas in Pakistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Omar" title="Mohammed Omar">Mohammed Omar</a>, the movement spread throughout most of Afghanistan, sequestering power from the <a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">Mujahideen</a> warlords. The <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan</a> was established in 1996 and the Afghan capital transferred to <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>. It held control of most of the country until being overthrown by the American-led <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%9314)" class="mw-redirect" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–14)">invasion of Afghanistan</a> in December 2001 following the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>. At its peak, formal <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_recognition" title="Diplomatic recognition">diplomatic recognition</a> of the Taliban's government was acknowledged by only three nations: <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>. The group later regrouped as <a href="/wiki/Taliban_insurgency" title="Taliban insurgency">an insurgency</a> movement to fight the American-backed <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Hamid_Karzai" title="Presidency of Hamid Karzai">Karzai administration</a> and the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>-led <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">International Security Assistance Force</a> (ISAF).</p>
<p>The Taliban have been condemned internationally for the harsh enforcement of their interpretation of Islamic <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> law, which has resulted in the brutal treatment of many Afghans, especially <a href="/wiki/Taliban_treatment_of_women" title="Taliban treatment of women">women</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Skain_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skain-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> During their rule from 1996 to 2001, the Taliban and their allies committed massacres against Afghan civilians, denied UN food supplies to 160,000 starving civilians and conducted a policy of <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a>, burning vast areas of fertile land and destroying tens of thousands of homes.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid2_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid2-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Newsday_2001_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newsday_2001-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-papillonsartpalace.com_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-papillonsartpalace.com-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NPR_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-45">[45]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the Taliban and their allies were responsible for 76% of Afghan <a href="/wiki/Civilian_casualties_in_the_war_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="Civilian casualties in the war in Afghanistan (2001–present)">civilian casualties</a> in 2010, 80% in 2011, and 80% in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Skaine_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skaine-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shanty1_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shanty1-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UNAMA_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNAMA-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haddon_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haddon-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Weekly_Standard_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Weekly_Standard-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Taliban's ideology has been described as combining an "innovative form" of <i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a></i> Islamic law based on <a href="/wiki/Deobandi" title="Deobandi">Deobandi</a> <a href="/wiki/Fundamentalism" title="Fundamentalism">fundamentalism</a><sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_2000_132.2C_139_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid_2000_132.2C_139-52">[52]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Militant_Islamism" class="mw-redirect" title="Militant Islamism">militant Islamism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salafi_jihadism" title="Salafi jihadism">Salafi jihadism</a> of Osama bin Laden<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_2000_132.2C_139_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid_2000_132.2C_139-52">[52]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtun</a> social and cultural norms known as <a href="/wiki/Pashtunwali" title="Pashtunwali">Pashtunwali</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Giustozzi_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giustozzi-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clements0_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clements0-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maley2_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maley2-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shaffer_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaffer-54">[54]</a></sup> as most Taliban are <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtun</a> tribesmen.</p>
<p>The Pakistani <a href="/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence" title="Inter-Services Intelligence">Inter-Services Intelligence</a> and military are widely alleged by the international community to have provided support to the Taliban during their founding and time in power, and of continuing to support the Taliban during the insurgency. <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> states that it dropped all support for the group after the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>. <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> also supported the Taliban with fighters from Arab countries and Central Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Giraldo_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giraldo-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch-PST_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch-PST-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-longwarjournal1-quote_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longwarjournal1-quote-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barnes_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_2008-09-09_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2008-09-09-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>Taliban</i> is <a href="/wiki/Pashto" title="Pashto">Pashto</a>, <span lang="ps" xml:lang="ps">طالبان</span> <i><span title="Pashto transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">ṭālibān</span></i>, meaning "students", the plural of <i><a href="/wiki/Talibe" title="Talibe">ṭālib</a></i>. This is a <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanword</a> from <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">طالب</span> <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">ṭālib</span></i>, using the <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> plural ending <i>-ān</i> <span lang="ps" xml:lang="ps">ان</span>. In Arabic <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">طالبان</span> <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">ṭālibān</span></i> means not "students" but "two students", as it is a "dual form", the Arabic plural being <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">طلاب</span> <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">ṭullāb</span></i>—occasionally causing some confusion to Arabic speakers. Since becoming a loanword in English, <i>Taliban</i>, besides a plural noun referring to the group, has also been used as a singular noun referring to an individual. For example, <a href="/wiki/John_Walker_Lindh" title="John Walker Lindh">John Walker Lindh</a> has been referred to as "an American Taliban", rather than "an American Talib". In the English language newspapers of Pakistan, the word <i>Talibans</i> is often used when referring to more than one Taliban. The spelling <i>Taliban</i> has come to be predominant over <i>Taleban</i> in English.<sup id="cite_ref-Arabic_Dictionary_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arabic_Dictionary-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Soviet_intervention_(1978–92)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_intervention_.281978.E2.80.9392.29">Soviet intervention (1978–92)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg/220px-Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg/330px-Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg/440px-Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1996" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> meeting with <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Mujahideen" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan Mujahideen">Afghan Mujahideen</a> leaders in the Oval Office in 1983</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(1978%E2%80%93present)#Soviet_intervention_and_withdrawal" title="War in Afghanistan (1978–present)">the Soviet Union intervened and occupied Afghanistan in 1979</a>, Islamic <a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">mujahideen</a> fighters engaged in war with those Soviet forces.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>'s President <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Zia-ul-Haq" title="Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq">Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq</a> feared that the Soviets were planning to invade also <a href="/wiki/Balochistan,_Pakistan" title="Balochistan, Pakistan">Balochistan</a>, Pakistan, so he sent <a href="/wiki/Akhtar_Abdur_Rahman" title="Akhtar Abdur Rahman">Akhtar Abdur Rahman</a> to <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> to garner support for the Afghan resistance against Soviet occupation forces. A while later, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> and Saudi Arabic General Intelligence Directorate (GID) funneled funding and equipment through the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>I <a href="/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence" title="Inter-Services Intelligence">Inter-Service Intelligence Agency</a> (ISI) to the Afghan mujahideen.<sup id="cite_ref-Price_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 90,000 Afghans, including <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Omar" title="Mohammed Omar">Mohammed Omar</a>, were trained by Pakistan's ISI during the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Price_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price-63">[63]</a></sup> The renowned British Professor <a href="/wiki/Carole_Hillenbrand" title="Carole Hillenbrand">Carole Hillenbrand</a> concluded that the Taliban have arisen from those US-Saudi-Pakistan-supported mujahideen: "The West helped the Taliban to fight the Soviet takeover of Afghanistan".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Afghan_Civil_War_(1992–96)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Afghan_Civil_War_.281992.E2.80.9396.29">Afghan Civil War (1992–96)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">Afghan Civil War (1992–96)</a></div>
<p>After the fall of the Soviet-backed regime of <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Najibullah" title="Mohammad Najibullah">Mohammad Najibullah</a> in 1992, many Afghan political parties, but not <a href="/wiki/Gulbuddin_Hekmatyar" title="Gulbuddin Hekmatyar">Gulbuddin Hekmatyar</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hezb-e_Islami" class="mw-redirect" title="Hezb-e Islami">Hezb-e Islami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hizb-e_Wahdat" class="mw-redirect" title="Hizb-e Wahdat">Hizb-e Wahdat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ittihad-i_Islami" class="mw-redirect" title="Ittihad-i Islami">Ittihad-i Islami</a>, in April agreed on a peace and power-sharing agreement, the <a href="/wiki/Peshawar_Accord" title="Peshawar Accord">Peshawar Accord</a>, which created the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic State of Afghanistan">Islamic State of Afghanistan</a> and appointed an interim government for a transitional period.</p>
<p>Hekmatyar's <a href="/wiki/Hezb-e_Islami" class="mw-redirect" title="Hezb-e Islami">Hezb-e Islami</a> party refused to recognize the interim government, and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)#Timeline" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">in April infiltrated Kabul to take power for itself, thus starting this civil war</a>. In May, Hekmatyar started attacks against government forces and <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29-65">[65]</a></sup> Hekmatyar received operational, financial and military support from Pakistan's ISI.<sup id="cite_ref-Neamatollah_Nojumi_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neamatollah_Nojumi-66">[66]</a></sup> With that help, Hekmatyar's forces were able to destroy half of Kabul.<sup id="cite_ref-Amin_Saikal_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amin_Saikal-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> assisted the <a href="/wiki/Hizb-e_Wahdat" class="mw-redirect" title="Hizb-e Wahdat">Hizb-e Wahdat</a> forces of <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Ali_Mazari" title="Abdul Ali Mazari">Abdul Ali Mazari</a>. <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> supported the <a href="/wiki/Ittihad-i_Islami" class="mw-redirect" title="Ittihad-i Islami">Ittihad-i Islami</a> faction.<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Amin_Saikal_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amin_Saikal-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roy_Gutman_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_Gutman-68">[68]</a></sup> The conflict between these militias also escalated into war.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_a_section_of_Kandahar_in_2013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Aerial_view_of_a_section_of_Kandahar_in_2013.jpg/220px-Aerial_view_of_a_section_of_Kandahar_in_2013.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Aerial_view_of_a_section_of_Kandahar_in_2013.jpg/330px-Aerial_view_of_a_section_of_Kandahar_in_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Aerial_view_of_a_section_of_Kandahar_in_2013.jpg/440px-Aerial_view_of_a_section_of_Kandahar_in_2013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_a_section_of_Kandahar_in_2013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Taliban emerged in the southern Afghan city of <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a> around September 1994.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to this sudden initiation of civil war, working government departments, police units or a system of justice and accountability for the newly created <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic State of Afghanistan">Islamic State of Afghanistan</a> did not have time to form. Horrific crimes were committed by individuals inside different factions. Ceasefires, negotiated by representatives of the Islamic State's newly appointed Defense Minister <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Massoud" title="Ahmad Shah Massoud">Ahmad Shah Massoud</a>, President <a href="/wiki/Sibghatullah_Mojaddedi" title="Sibghatullah Mojaddedi">Sibghatullah Mojaddedi</a> and later President <a href="/wiki/Burhanuddin_Rabbani" title="Burhanuddin Rabbani">Burhanuddin Rabbani</a> (the interim government), or officials from the International Committee of the Red Cross (ICRC), commonly collapsed within days.<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29-65">[65]</a></sup> The countryside in northern Afghanistan, parts of which was under the control of Defense Minister Massoud remained calm and some reconstruction took place. The city of Herat under the rule of Islamic State ally <a href="/wiki/Ismail_Khan" title="Ismail Khan">Ismail Khan</a> also witnessed relative calm.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, southern Afghanistan was neither under the control of foreign-backed militias nor the government in Kabul, but was ruled by local leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Gul_Agha_Sherzai" title="Gul Agha Sherzai">Gul Agha Sherzai</a> and their militias. The Taliban only first <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)#July.E2.80.93December" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">emerged on the scene in August 1994</a>, announcing to liberate Afghanistan from its present corrupt leadership of warlords, and establish a pure Islamic society.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Taliban%27s_rise_to_power" title="Taliban's rise to power">Taliban's rise to power</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1994">1994</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)#1994" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">Afghan Civil War (1992–96) §&#160;1994</a></div>
<p>In 1994, the Taliban – a movement originating from <a href="/wiki/Jamiat_Ulema-e-Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamiat Ulema-e-Islam">Jamiat Ulema-e-Islam</a>-run religious schools for Afghan refugees in Pakistan – developed in Afghanistan as a politico-religious force.<sup id="cite_ref-Matinuddin.2C_Kamal_1999_pp.25_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matinuddin.2C_Kamal_1999_pp.25-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Omar" title="Mohammed Omar">Mullah Omar</a> started his Taliban movement with fewer than 50 armed <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrassah</a> students in his hometown of <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>. Two glorifying stories about the early Taliban, told by Taliban, run like this:<br />
In the spring of 1994, neighbors in <a href="/wiki/Singesar" title="Singesar">Singesar</a> told <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Omar" title="Mohammed Omar">Mullah Omar</a> that the local governor had abducted two teenage girls, shaved their heads, and taken them to a camp where they were raped. 30 Taliban (with only 16 rifles) freed the girls, and hanged the governor from the barrel of a tank. Later that year, two militia commanders killed civilians while fighting for the right to <a href="/wiki/Sodomize" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodomize">sodomize</a> a young boy: the Taliban freed the boy.<sup id="cite_ref-Matinuddin.2C_Kamal_1999_pp.25_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matinuddin.2C_Kamal_1999_pp.25-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early 1994, 15,000 students from the madrassas in Pakistan joined the Taliban. In August 1994, the Taliban marched northward from <a href="/wiki/Maywand_District" title="Maywand District">Maiwand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kandahar#Modern_history" title="Kandahar">captured Kandahar City and the surrounding provinces</a>, losing only a few dozen men. They forced the surrender of dozens of local Pashtun leaders who had presided over a situation of complete lawlessness and atrocities. The Taliban also took over a border crossing at Spin Baldak and an ammunition dump from <a href="/wiki/Gulbuddin_Hekmatyar" title="Gulbuddin Hekmatyar">Gulbuddin Hekmatyar</a>.</p>
<p>By October 1994, the Taliban had acquired the support of Pakistan which was unhappy with the unsuccessful Hekmatyar and saw in the Taliban a way to secure trade routes to Central Asia and establish a government in Kabul friendly to its interests (see: <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)#1994" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">Afghan Civil War (1992–96)#1994</a>).</p>
<p>Late 1994, most of the <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan#Civil_war" title="Afghanistan">militia factions (Hekmatyar's Hezb-i Islami, Dostum's Junbish-i Milli and Hezb-i Wahdat) fighting in the battle for control of Kabul were defeated</a> militarily by forces of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic State of Afghanistan">Islamic State of Afghanistan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Defense_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Defense Minister">Defense Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Massoud" title="Ahmad Shah Massoud">Ahmad Shah Massoud</a>. The Islamic State initiated measures to restore law and order to the capital Kabul, and initiate a nationwide political process for national <a href="/wiki/Democratic_consolidation" title="Democratic consolidation">democratic consolidation</a> and democratic elections. Hoping for the Taliban to be allies in bringing stability to Afghanistan, Massoud invited the Taliban to join the consolidation process and to contribute to stability. Unarmed, Massoud went to talk to Taliban leaders in <a href="/wiki/Maidan_Shar" title="Maidan Shar">Maidan Shar</a> to convince them to join the initiated political process, so that democratic elections could be held to decide on a future government for Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Afghanistan_Justice_Project_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Afghanistan_Justice_Project-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-amnesty.org_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty.org-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Charlie_Rose_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charlie_Rose-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Taliban declined to join such a political process. When Massoud returned unharmed to Kabul, the Taliban leader who had received him as his guest was killed by other senior Taliban for failing to execute Massoud while the possibility had presented itself.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Before 4 January 1995, the Taliban took control of 12 of 34 <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Afghanistan" title="Provinces of Afghanistan">provinces</a><sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford-77">[77]</a></sup> not under central government control; militias controlling the different areas often surrendered without a fight. Omar's commanders were a mixture of former small-unit military commanders and madrassa teachers.<sup id="cite_ref-Felbab-Brow_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Felbab-Brow-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_2000_27.E2.80.9329_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid_2000_27.E2.80.9329-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harvnb.7CRashid.7C2000.7Cp.3D29_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvnb.7CRashid.7C2000.7Cp.3D29-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="1995–September_1996"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1995.E2.80.93September_1996">1995–September 1996</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)#1995" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">Afghan Civil War (1992–96) §&#160;1995</a></div>
<p>In a bid to establish their rule over all Afghanistan, the Taliban started shelling <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a> in early 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-amnesty.org_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty.org-73">[73]</a></sup> The Taliban suffered a devastating defeat against government forces of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic State of Afghanistan">Islamic State of Afghanistan</a> under the command of Ahmad Shah Massoud.</p>
<p>Pakistan, however, started to provide stronger military support to the Taliban. On September 26, 1996, as the Taliban with military support by Pakistan and financial support by Saudi Arabia prepared for another major offensive, Massoud ordered a full retreat from Kabul to continue anti-Taliban resistance in the northeastern <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> mountains instead of engaging in street battles in Kabul. The Taliban entered Kabul on September 27, 1996, and established the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan</a>. Analysts described the Taliban then as developing into a <a href="/wiki/Proxy_war" title="Proxy war">proxy</a> force for Pakistan's regional interests,<sup id="cite_ref-Amin_Saikal_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amin_Saikal-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-amnesty.org_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty.org-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-George_Washington_University_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-George_Washington_University-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_politisch_1996.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Afghanistan_politisch_1996.png/220px-Afghanistan_politisch_1996.png" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Afghanistan_politisch_1996.png/330px-Afghanistan_politisch_1996.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Afghanistan_politisch_1996.png/440px-Afghanistan_politisch_1996.png 2x" data-file-width="2079" data-file-height="1624" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_politisch_1996.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing political control in Afghanistan in the fall of 1996, following the capture of Kabul by the Taliban.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>as had been Pakistan's intention in October 1994 (see above).</p>
<h3><span id="Taliban_rule_(1996-2001)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Taliban_rule_.281996-2001.29">Taliban rule (1996-2001)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1996%E2%80%932001)" title="Afghan Civil War (1996–2001)">Afghan Civil War (1996–2001)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>The military goal of the Taliban during the period 1995 to 2001 was to return the order of <a href="/wiki/Abdur_Rahman_Khan" title="Abdur Rahman Khan">Abdur Rahman</a> (<i>the Iron <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">Emir</a></i>) by the re-establishment of a state with <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtun</a> dominance within the northern areas.<sup id="cite_ref-B.G._Williams_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B.G._Williams-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Afghanistan_during_Taliban_rule">Afghanistan during Taliban rule</span></h4>
<p>When the Taliban took power in 1996, <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(1978%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (1978–present)">twenty years of continuous warfare</a> had devastated Afghanistan's <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> and economy. There was no running water, little electricity, few telephones, functioning roads or regular energy supplies. Basic necessities like water, food, housing and others were in desperately short supply. In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Clan" title="Clan">clan</a> and family structure that provided Afghans with a social/economic safety net was also badly damaged. Afghanistan's infant mortality was the highest in the world. A full quarter of all children died before they reached their fifth birthday, a rate several times higher than most other developing countries.<sup id="cite_ref-rashid_107_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rashid_107-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>International charitable and/or development organisations (<a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organizations</a> or NGOs) were extremely important to the supply of food, employment, reconstruction, and other services, but the Taliban proved highly suspicious towards the 'help' those organizations offered (see <a href="/wiki/Taliban#United_Nations_and_NGOs" title="Taliban">§ United Nations and NGOs</a>). With one million plus deaths during the years of war, the number of families headed by widows had reached 98,000 by 1998. In Kabul, where vast portions of the city had been devastated from rocket attacks, more than half of its 1.2 million people benefited in some way from NGO activities, even for water to drink. The <a href="/wiki/Civil_war_in_Afghanistan_(1996%E2%80%932001)" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil war in Afghanistan (1996–2001)">civil war</a> and its never-ending refugee stream continued throughout the Taliban's reign. The Mazar, Herat, and Shomali valley offensives displaced more than three-quarters of a million civilians, using "<a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a>" tactics to prevent them from supplying the enemy with aid.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Taliban decision-makers, particularly Mullah Omar, seldom if ever talked directly to non-Muslim foreigners, so aid providers had to deal with intermediaries whose approvals and agreements were often reversed.<sup id="cite_ref-rashid_101-102_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rashid_101-102-93">[93]</a></sup> Around September 1997 the heads of three UN agencies in Kandahar were expelled from the country after protesting when a female <a href="/wiki/Lawyer" title="Lawyer">attorney</a> for the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" title="United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees">UN High Commissioner for Refugees</a> was forced to talk from behind a curtain so her face would not be visible.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the UN increased the number of Muslim women staff to satisfy Taliban demands, the Taliban then required all female Muslim UN staff traveling to Afghanistan to be chaperoned by a <a href="/wiki/Mahram" title="Mahram">mahram</a> or a blood relative.<sup id="cite_ref-taliban71_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taliban71-95">[95]</a></sup> In July 1998, the Taliban closed "all NGO offices" by force after those organizations refused to move to a bombed-out former <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_technology" title="Institute of technology">Polytechnic</a> College as ordered.<sup id="cite_ref-bmj_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmj-96">[96]</a></sup> One month later the UN offices were also shut down.<sup id="cite_ref-rashid.2C71_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rashid.2C71-97">[97]</a></sup> As food prices rose and conditions deteriorated, Planning Minister Qari Din Mohammed explained the Taliban's indifference to the loss of humanitarian aid:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We Muslims believe God the Almighty will feed everybody one way or another. If the foreign NGOs leave then it is their decision. We have not expelled them.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_the_Pakistani_military">Role of the Pakistani military</span></h4>
<p>The Taliban were largely founded by Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence" title="Inter-Services Intelligence">Inter-Services Intelligence</a> (ISI) during 1994. The I.S.I. used the Taliban to establish a regime in Afghanistan which would be favorable to Pakistan, as they were trying to gain <a href="/wiki/Strategic_depth" title="Strategic depth">strategic depth</a>. Since the creation of the Taliban, the ISI and the Pakistani military have given financial, logistical and military support.<sup id="cite_ref-Giraldo_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giraldo-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shaffer2_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaffer2-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsythe3_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsythe3-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-.5B.5BHall_Gardner.5D.5D_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.5B.5BHall_Gardner.5D.5D-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jones_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Randal_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randal-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peiman_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peiman-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hilali_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilali-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rumer_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rumer-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pape_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pape-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harf_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harf-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hinnells_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinnells-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boase_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boase-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Armajani-48_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armajani-48-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bayo_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayo-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goodson_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodson-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Pakistani Afghanistan expert <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Rashid" title="Ahmed Rashid">Ahmed Rashid</a>, "between 1994 and 1999, an estimated 80,000 to 100,000 Pakistanis trained and fought in Afghanistan" on the side of the Taliban. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Tomsen" title="Peter Tomsen">Peter Tomsen</a> stated that up until 9/11 Pakistani military and ISI officers along with thousands of regular Pakistani armed forces personnel had been involved in the fighting in Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-Maley_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maley-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tomsen-322_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tomsen-322-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 2001, according to several international sources, 28,000–30,000 Pakistani nationals, 14,000–15,000 Afghan Taliban and 2,000–3,000 <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> militants were fighting against anti-Taliban forces in Afghanistan as a roughly 45,000 strong military force. Pakistani President <a href="/wiki/Pervez_Musharraf" title="Pervez Musharraf">Pervez Musharraf</a> – then as Chief of Army Staff – was responsible for sending thousands of Pakistanis to fight alongside the Taliban and Bin Laden against the forces of Ahmad Shah Massoud. Of the estimated 28,000 Pakistani nationals fighting in Afghanistan, 8,000 were militants recruited in <a href="/wiki/Madrassa" class="mw-redirect" title="Madrassa">madrassas</a> filling regular Taliban ranks. The document further states that the parents of those Pakistani nationals "know nothing regarding their child's military involvement with the Taliban until their bodies are brought back to Pakistan." A 1998 document by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._State_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. State Department">U.S. State Department</a> confirms that "20–40 percent of [regular] Taliban soldiers are Pakistani." According to the U.S. State Department report and reports by Human Rights Watch, the other Pakistani nationals fighting in Afghanistan were regular Pakistani soldiers, especially from the <a href="/wiki/Frontier_Corps" title="Frontier Corps">Frontier Corps</a> but also from the army providing direct combat support.<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-George_Washington_University_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-George_Washington_University-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch-PST-no-quote_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch-PST-no-quote-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Edward_Girardet_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_Girardet-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_2000_91_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid_2000_91-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-National_Geographic_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_Geographic-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Human Rights Watch wrote in 2000:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Of all the foreign powers involved in efforts to sustain and manipulate the ongoing fighting [in Afghanistan], Pakistan is distinguished both by the sweep of its objectives and the scale of its efforts, which include soliciting funding for the Taliban, bankrolling Taliban operations, providing diplomatic support as the Taliban's virtual emissaries abroad, arranging training for Taliban fighters, recruiting skilled and unskilled manpower to serve in Taliban armies, planning and directing offensives, providing and facilitating shipments of ammunition and fuel, and ... directly providing combat support.<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch-PST_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch-PST-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>On August 1, 1997 the Taliban launched an attack on Sheberghan, the main military base of Abdul Rashid Dostum. Dostum has said the reason the attack was successful was due to 1500 Pakistani commandos taking part and that the Pakistani air force also gave support.<sup id="cite_ref-Clements2_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clements2-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1998, Iran accused Pakistan of sending its air force to bomb <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-i-Sharif</a> in support of Taliban forces and directly accused Pakistani troops for "war crimes at <a href="/wiki/Bamiyan" class="mw-redirect" title="Bamiyan">Bamiyan</a>". The same year, Russia said Pakistan was responsible for the "military expansion" of the Taliban in northern Afghanistan by sending large numbers of Pakistani troops, some of whom had subsequently been taken as prisoners by the anti-Taliban United Front.<sup id="cite_ref-Washington_Post_.282.29_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Washington_Post_.282.29-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Press_Trust_of_India_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Press_Trust_of_India-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 2000, the UN Security Council imposed an arms embargo against military support to the Taliban, with UN officials explicitly singling out Pakistan. The UN secretary-general implicitly criticized Pakistan for its military support and the Security Council stated it was "deeply distress[ed] over reports of involvement in the fighting, on the Taliban side, of thousands of non-Afghan nationals." In July 2001, several countries, including the United States, accused Pakistan of being "in violation of U.N. sanctions because of its military aid to the Taliban." The Taliban also obtained financial resources from Pakistan. In 1997 alone, after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kabul_(1992%E2%80%9396)" title="Battle of Kabul (1992–96)">capture of Kabul</a> by the Taliban, Pakistan gave $30 million in aid and a further $10 million for government wages.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Washington_Times_.282.29_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Washington_Times_.282.29-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Byman_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Byman-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 2000, <a href="/wiki/Secret_Intelligence_Service" title="Secret Intelligence Service">British Intelligence</a> reported that the ISI was taking an active role in several <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> training camps. The ISI helped with the construction of training camps for both the Taliban and Al-Qaeda. From 1996 to 2001 the <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> of <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayman_al-Zawahiri" title="Ayman al-Zawahiri">Ayman al-Zawahiri</a> became a state within the Taliban state. Bin Laden sent Arab and Central Asian Al-Qaeda militants to join the fight against the United Front, among them his <a href="/wiki/055_Brigade" title="055 Brigade">Brigade 055</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Atkins_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Litwak_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Litwak-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McGrath_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Daily_Times_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Times-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the 9/11 attacks, Pakistan claimed to have ended its support to the Taliban. But with the fall of Kabul to anti-Taliban forces in November 2001, ISI forces worked with and helped Taliban militias who were in full retreat. In November 2001, Taliban, Al-Qaeda combatants and ISI operatives were safely evacuated from Kunduz on <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Army" title="Pakistan Army">Pakistan Army</a> cargo aircraft to <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Air_Force" title="Pakistan Air Force">Pakistan Air Force</a> bases in <a href="/wiki/Chitral" title="Chitral">Chitral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gilgit" title="Gilgit">Gilgit</a> in Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Northern_Areas" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Areas">Northern Areas</a> in what has been dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Kunduz_airlift" title="Kunduz airlift">"Airlift of Evil."</a> Former Pakistani president Pervez Musharraf wrote in his memoirs that <a href="/wiki/Richard_Armitage_(politician)" title="Richard Armitage (politician)">Richard Armitage</a>, the former US deputy secretary of state, said Pakistan would be "bombed back to the stone-age" if it continued to support the Taliban, although Armitage has since denied using the "stone age" phrase.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansford-37_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansford-37-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lall_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lall-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-www_newyorker_com10_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www_newyorker_com10-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morgan_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-135">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Musharraf_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Musharraf-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gartenstein-Ross_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gartenstein-Ross-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hansen_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Riedel-65_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedel-65-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>The role of the Pakistani military has been described by international observers as well as by the anti-Taliban leader Ahmad Shah Massoud as a "creeping invasion". Yet the "creeping invasion" proved unable to defeat the severely outnumbered anti-Taliban forces.<sup id="cite_ref-Maley_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maley-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pakistan has been accused of continuing to support the Taliban since 9/11, an allegation Pakistan denies.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CQ_Researcher_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CQ_Researcher-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-Taliban_resistance_under_Massoud">Anti-Taliban resistance under Massoud</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_politisch_2000.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Afghanistan_politisch_2000.png/220px-Afghanistan_politisch_2000.png" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Afghanistan_politisch_2000.png/330px-Afghanistan_politisch_2000.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Afghanistan_politisch_2000.png/440px-Afghanistan_politisch_2000.png 2x" data-file-width="2079" data-file-height="1624" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_politisch_2000.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Alliance" title="Northern Alliance">Northern Alliance</a> in the spring of 2000, when the Taliban was at the height of its power.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Massoud" title="Ahmad Shah Massoud">Ahmad Shah Massoud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rashid_Dostum" title="Abdul Rashid Dostum">Abdul Rashid Dostum</a>, former enemies, created the United Front (<a href="/wiki/Northern_Alliance" title="Northern Alliance">Northern Alliance</a>) against the Taliban that were preparing offensives against the remaining areas under the control of Massoud and those under the control of Dostum. The United Front included beside the dominantly <a href="/wiki/Tajiks" title="Tajiks">Tajik</a> forces of Massoud and the <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbek</a> forces of Dostum, <a href="/wiki/Hazaras" title="Hazaras">Hazara</a> troops led by <a href="/wiki/Haji_Mohammad_Mohaqiq" class="mw-redirect" title="Haji Mohammad Mohaqiq">Haji Mohammad Mohaqiq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtun</a> forces under the leadership of commanders such as <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Haq_(Afghan_leader)" title="Abdul Haq (Afghan leader)">Abdul Haq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haji_Abdul_Qadir" class="mw-redirect" title="Haji Abdul Qadir">Haji Abdul Qadir</a>. Notable politicians and diplomats of the United Front included <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rahim_Ghafoorzai" title="Abdul Rahim Ghafoorzai">Abdul Rahim Ghafoorzai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Abdullah" title="Abdullah Abdullah">Abdullah Abdullah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Massoud_Khalili" title="Massoud Khalili">Massoud Khalili</a>. From the Taliban conquest of Kabul in September 1996 until November 2001 the United Front controlled roughly 30% of Afghanistan's population in provinces such as <a href="/wiki/Badakhshan_Province" title="Badakhshan Province">Badakhshan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kapisa_Province" title="Kapisa Province">Kapisa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Takhar_Province" title="Takhar Province">Takhar</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Parwan_Province" title="Parwan Province">Parwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunar_Province" title="Kunar Province">Kunar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuristan_Province" title="Nuristan Province">Nuristan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laghman_Province" title="Laghman Province">Laghman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samangan_Province" title="Samangan Province">Samangan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunduz_Province" title="Kunduz Province">Kunduz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghor_Province" title="Ghor Province">Ghōr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bamyan_Province" title="Bamyan Province">Bamyan</a>.</p>
<p>After longstanding battles, especially for the northern city of <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-i-Sharif</a>, Abdul Rashid Dostum and his Junbish forces were defeated by the Taliban and their allies in 1998. Dostum subsequently went into exile. Ahmad Shah Massoud remained the only major anti-Taliban leader inside Afghanistan who was able to defend vast parts of his territory against the Taliban.</p>
<p>In the areas under his control Massoud set up democratic institutions and signed the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Women's Rights">Women's Rights</a> Declaration. In the area of Massoud, women and girls did not have to wear the Afghan burqa. They were allowed to work and to go to school. In at least two known instances, Massoud personally intervened against cases of forced marriage.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is our conviction and we believe that both men and women are created by the Almighty. Both have equal rights. Women can pursue an education, women can pursue a career, and women can play a role in society – just like men.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Ahmad Shah Massoud, 2001</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Massoud is adamant that in Afghanistan women have suffered oppression for generations. He says that 'the cultural environment of the country suffocates women. But the Taliban exacerbate this with oppression.' His most ambitious project is to shatter this cultural prejudice and so give more space, freedom and equality to women – they would have the same rights as men.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Pepe Escobar, Massoud: From Warrior to Statesman</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Afghan traditions would need a generation or more to overcome and could only be challenged by education, he said. Humayun Tandar, who took part as an Afghan diplomat in the 2001 <a href="/wiki/International_Conference_on_Afghanistan,_Bonn_(2001)" title="International Conference on Afghanistan, Bonn (2001)">International Conference on Afghanistan</a> in Bonn, said that "strictures of language, ethnicity, region were [also] stifling for Massoud. That is why ... he wanted to create a unity which could surpass the situation in which we found ourselves and still find ourselves to this day." This applied also to strictures of religion. Jean-José Puig describes how Massoud often led prayers before a meal or at times asked his fellow Muslims to lead the prayer but also did not hesitate to ask a Christian friend Jean-José Puig or the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> Professor Michael Barry: "Jean-José, we believe in the same God. Please, tell us the prayer before lunch or dinner in your own language."<sup id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_71-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Human Rights Watch cites no human rights crimes for the forces under direct control of Massoud for the period from October 1996 until the assassination of Massoud in September 2001. 400,000 to one million Afghans fled from the Taliban to the area of Massoud.<sup id="cite_ref-National_Geographic_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_Geographic-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch-backgrounder-2001_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch-backgrounder-2001-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic</a> concluded in its documentary <i>"Inside the Taliban"</i>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The only thing standing in the way of future Taliban massacres is Ahmad Shah Massoud.<sup id="cite_ref-National_Geographic_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_Geographic-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— National Geographic, Inside the Taliban</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The Taliban repeatedly offered Massoud a position of power to make him stop his resistance. Massoud declined. He explained in one interview:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Taliban say: 'Come and accept the post of prime minister and be with us', and they would keep the highest office in the country, the presidentship. But at what cost?! The difference between us concerns mainly our way of thinking about the very principles of the society and the state. We can not accept their conditions of compromise, or else we would have to give up the principles of modern democracy. We are fundamentally against the system called 'the Emirate of Afghanistan".<sup id="cite_ref-Interview_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Interview-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Ahmad Shah Massoud, 2001</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The United Front in its <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120310123118/http://www.peace-initiatives.com/frame.htm">Proposals for Peace</a> demanded the Taliban to join a political process leading towards nationwide democratic elections. In early 2001, Massoud employed a new strategy of local military pressure and global political appeals. Resentment was increasingly gathering against Taliban rule from the bottom of Afghan society, including the Pashtun areas. Massoud publicized their cause of "popular consensus, general elections and democracy" worldwide. At the same time he was very wary not to revive the failed Kabul government of the early 1990s. Already in 1999, he started the training of police forces which he trained specifically in order to keep order and protect the civilian population in case the United Front would be successful.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_71-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Interview_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Interview-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steve_Coll:_Ghost_Wars_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steve_Coll:_Ghost_Wars-145">[145]</a></sup> Massoud stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Taliban are not a force to be considered invincible. They are distanced from the people now. They are weaker than in the past. There is only the assistance given by Pakistan, Osama bin Laden and other extremist groups that keep the Taliban on their feet. With a halt to that assistance, it is extremely difficult to survive.<sup id="cite_ref-St._Petersburg_Times_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-St._Petersburg_Times-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Ahmad Shah Massoud, 2001</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>From 1999 onwards, a renewed process was set into motion by the Tajik Ahmad Shah Massoud and the Pashtun Abdul Haq to unite all the ethnicities of Afghanistan. While Massoud united the Tajiks, Hazara and Uzbeks as well as some Pashtun commanders under his United Front command, the famed Pashtun commander <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Haq_(Afghan_leader)" title="Abdul Haq (Afghan leader)">Abdul Haq</a> received increasing numbers of defecting Pashtun Taliban as "Taliban popularity trended downward". Both agreed to work together with the exiled Afghan king <a href="/wiki/Zahir_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Zahir Shah">Zahir Shah</a>. International officials who met with representatives of the new alliance, which Pulitzer Prize winner <a href="/wiki/Steve_Coll" title="Steve Coll">Steve Coll</a> referred to as the "grand Pashtun-Tajik alliance", said, "It's crazy that you have this today ... Pashtuns, Tajiks, Uzbeks, Hazara ... They were all ready to buy in to the process ... to work under the king's banner for an ethnically balanced Afghanistan." Senior diplomat and Afghanistan expert <a href="/wiki/Peter_Tomsen" title="Peter Tomsen">Peter Tomsen</a> wrote: "The ‘Lion of Kabul’ [Abdul Haq] and the ‘Lion of Panjshir’ [Ahmad Shah Massoud] ... Haq, Massoud, and Karzai, Afghanistan’s three leading moderates, could transcend the Pashtun—non-Pashtun, north-south divide." The most senior Hazara and Uzbek leader were also part of the process. In late 2000, Massoud officially brought together this new alliance in a meeting in Northern Afghanistan to discuss, among other things, "a Loya Jirga, or a traditional council of elders, to settle political turmoil in Afghanistan". That part of the Pashtun-Tajik-Hazara-Uzbek peace plan did eventually materialize. An account of the meeting by author and journalist <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_Junger" title="Sebastian Junger">Sebastian Junger</a> says: "In 2000, when I was there ... I happened to be there in a very interesting time. ... Massoud brought together Afghan leaders from all ethnic groups. They flew from London, Paris, the USA, all parts of Afghanistan, Pakistan, India. He brought them all into the northern area where he was. He held a council of ... prominent Afghans from all over the world, brought there to discuss the Afghan government after the Taliban. ... we met all these men and interviewed them briefly. One was Hamid Karzai; I did not have any idea who he would end up being ..." <sup id="cite_ref-Steve_Coll:_Ghost_Wars_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steve_Coll:_Ghost_Wars-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tomsen-565_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tomsen-565-147">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_New_Statesman_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_Statesman-148">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Corbis_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corbis-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_2_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book_2-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 2001, Ahmad Shah Massoud with ethnic leaders from all of Afghanistan addressed the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> asking the <a href="/wiki/International_community" title="International community">international community</a> to provide <a href="/wiki/Humanitarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Humanitarian">humanitarian</a> help to the people of Afghanistan. He stated that the Taliban and <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> had introduced "a very wrong perception of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>" and that without the support of Pakistan and Bin Laden the Taliban would not be able to sustain their military campaign for up to a year. On this visit to Europe he also warned that his intelligence had gathered information about a large-scale attack on U.S. soil being imminent. The president of the European Parliament, <a href="/wiki/Nicole_Fontaine" title="Nicole Fontaine">Nicole Fontaine</a>, called him the "pole of liberty in Afghanistan".<sup id="cite_ref-EU_Parliament_.282.29_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU_Parliament_.282.29-151">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EU_Parliament_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU_Parliament-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gwu.edu_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gwu.edu-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 9, 2001, Massoud, then aged 48, was the target of a <a href="/wiki/Suicide_attack" title="Suicide attack">suicide attack</a> by two Arabs posing as journalists at Khwaja Bahauddin, in the <a href="/wiki/Takhar_Province" title="Takhar Province">Takhar Province</a> of Afghanistan. Massoud, who had survived countless assassination attempts over a period of 26 years, died in a helicopter taking him to a hospital. The first attempt on Massoud's life had been carried out by Hekmatyar and two Pakistani ISI agents in 1975, when Massoud was only 22 years old. In early 2001, Al-Qaeda would-be assassins were captured by Massoud's forces while trying to enter his territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Roy_Gutman_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_Gutman-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steve_Coll:_Ghost_Wars_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steve_Coll:_Ghost_Wars-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> The funeral, though in a rather rural area, was attended by hundreds of thousands of mourning people.</p>
<p>The assassination of Massoud is believed to have a connection to the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> on U.S. soil, which killed nearly 3000 people, and which appeared to be the terrorist attack that Massoud had warned against in his speech to the European Parliament several months earlier. <a href="/wiki/John_P._O%27Neill" title="John P. O'Neill">John P. O'Neill</a> was a counter-terrorism expert and the Assistant Director of the <a href="/wiki/FBI" class="mw-redirect" title="FBI">FBI</a> until late 2001. He retired from the FBI and was offered the position of director of security at the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center</a> (WTC). He took the job at the WTC two weeks before 9/11. On September 10, 2001, O'Neill told two of his friends, "We're due. And we're due for something big.... Some things have happened in Afghanistan. [referring to the assassination of Massoud] I don't like the way things are lining up in Afghanistan.... I sense a shift, and I think things are going to happen ... soon." O'Neill died on September 11, 2001, when the <a href="/wiki/Two_World_Trade_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Two World Trade Center">South Tower</a> collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PBS_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the terrorist attacks of September 11, 2001, Massoud's United Front troops and United Front troops of Abdul Rashid Dostum (who returned from exile) ousted the Taliban from power in Kabul with American air support in <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%9314)" class="mw-redirect" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–14)">Operation Enduring Freedom</a>. From October to December 2001, the United Front gained control of much of the country and played a crucial role in establishing the post-Taliban interim government under Hamid Karzai.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S.-led_overthrow_of_Taliban_and_further_battle_against_Taliban">U.S.-led overthrow of Taliban and further battle against Taliban</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">War in Afghanistan (2001–present)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taliban-Torkham-2001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Taliban-Torkham-2001.jpg/220px-Taliban-Torkham-2001.jpg" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Taliban-Torkham-2001.jpg/330px-Taliban-Torkham-2001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Taliban-Torkham-2001.jpg/440px-Taliban-Torkham-2001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2506" data-file-height="3777" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taliban-Torkham-2001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Taliban border guard in 2001</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude">Prelude</span></h4>
<p>On 20 September 2001, the U.S. president, speaking to a joint session of Congress, tentatively blamed Al-Qaeda for the September 11 attacks; the president stated that the "leadership of Al Qaeda ha[d] great influence in Afghanistan and support[ed] the Taliban regime in controlling most of that country". <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George Bush</a> then said: "We condemn the Taliban regime", and went on to state, "Tonight the United States of America makes the following demands on the Taliban", which he said were "not open to negotiation or discussion":<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<ol>
<li>Deliver to the U.S. all of the leaders of Al-Qaeda</li>
<li>Release all foreign nationals that have been unjustly imprisoned</li>
<li>Protect foreign journalists, diplomats, and aid workers</li>
<li>Close immediately every terrorist training camp</li>
<li>Hand over every terrorist and their supporters to appropriate authorities</li>
<li>Give the United States full access to terrorist training camps for inspection</li>
</ol>
</blockquote>
<p>The U.S. petitioned the international community to back a military campaign to overthrow the Taliban. The U.N. issued two resolutions on terrorism after the September 11 attacks. The resolutions called on all states to "[increase] cooperation and full implementation of the relevant international conventions relating to terrorism" and specified consensus recommendations for all countries. The Security Council did not authorize military intervention in Afghanistan of any kind, and nowhere in the U.N resolutions did it say military operations in Afghanistan were justified or conformed to international law. Despite this, NATO approved a campaign against Afghanistan as self-defense against armed attack.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Taliban ambassador to Pakistan, Abdul Salem Zaeef, responded to the ultimatum by demanding "convincing evidence" that Bin Laden was involved in the attacks, stating "our position is that if America has evidence and proof, they should produce it." Additionally, the Taliban insisted that any trial of Bin Laden be held in an Afghan court. Zaeef also claimed that "4,000 Jews working in the Trade Center had prior knowledge of the suicide missions, and 'were absent on that day.'" This response was generally dismissed as a delaying tactic, rather than a sincere attempt to cooperate with the ultimatum.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-163">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 22, the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, withdrew recognition of the Taliban as Afghanistan's legal government, leaving neighbouring Pakistan as the only remaining country with diplomatic ties. On October 4, the Taliban agreed to turn bin Laden over to Pakistan for trial in an international <a href="/wiki/Tribunal" title="Tribunal">tribunal</a> that operated according to Islamic <i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a></i> law, but Pakistan blocked the offer as it was not possible to guarantee his safety. On October 7, the Taliban ambassador to Pakistan offered to detain bin Laden and try him under Islamic law if the U.S. made a formal request and presented the Taliban with evidence. A Bush administration official, speaking on condition of anonymity, rejected the Taliban offer, and stated that the U.S. would not negotiate their demands.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coalition_attack">Coalition attack</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SF_Sgt_Mario_Vigil_with_SF_and_NA_forces_west_of_Konduz_in_November_2001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/SF_Sgt_Mario_Vigil_with_SF_and_NA_forces_west_of_Konduz_in_November_2001.jpg/220px-SF_Sgt_Mario_Vigil_with_SF_and_NA_forces_west_of_Konduz_in_November_2001.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/SF_Sgt_Mario_Vigil_with_SF_and_NA_forces_west_of_Konduz_in_November_2001.jpg/330px-SF_Sgt_Mario_Vigil_with_SF_and_NA_forces_west_of_Konduz_in_November_2001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/SF_Sgt_Mario_Vigil_with_SF_and_NA_forces_west_of_Konduz_in_November_2001.jpg/440px-SF_Sgt_Mario_Vigil_with_SF_and_NA_forces_west_of_Konduz_in_November_2001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SF_Sgt_Mario_Vigil_with_SF_and_NA_forces_west_of_Konduz_in_November_2001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Taliban were removed from power in October 2001 by a unified effort of <a href="/wiki/United_Islamic_Front" class="mw-redirect" title="United Islamic Front">United Islamic Front</a> (Northern Alliance) ground forces, small <a href="/wiki/United_States_special_operations_forces" title="United States special operations forces">U.S. Special Operations teams</a> and <a href="/wiki/USAF" class="mw-redirect" title="USAF">U.S. air support</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On October 7, less than one month after the September 11 attacks, the U.S., aided by the United Kingdom, Canada, and other countries including several from the NATO alliance, initiated <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">military action</a>, bombing Taliban and Al-Qaeda-related camps.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> The stated intent of military operations was to remove the Taliban from power, and prevent the use of Afghanistan as a <a href="/wiki/Terrorist" class="mw-redirect" title="Terrorist">terrorist</a> base of operations.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>The CIA's elite <a href="/wiki/Special_Activities_Division" title="Special Activities Division">Special Activities Division</a> (SAD) units were the first U.S. forces to enter Afghanistan (noting that many different countries intelligence agencies were on the ground or operating within theatre before SAD, and that SAD are not technically military forces, but civilian paramilitaries). They joined with the Afghan United Front (<a href="/wiki/Northern_Alliance" title="Northern Alliance">Northern Alliance</a>) to prepare for the subsequent arrival of U.S. Special Operations forces. The United Front (Northern Alliance) and SAD and <a href="/wiki/Special_Forces_(United_States_Army)" title="Special Forces (United States Army)">Special Forces</a> combined to overthrow the Taliban with minimal coalition casualties, and without the use of international conventional ground forces. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> stated in an editorial by John Lehman in 2006:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What made the Afghan campaign a landmark in the U.S. Military's history is that it was prosecuted by Special Operations forces from all the services, along with <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">Air Force</a> tactical power, operations by the Afghan Northern Alliance and the CIA were equally important and fully integrated. No large <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">Army</a> or <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Marine">Marine</a> force was employed.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>On October 14, the Taliban offered to discuss handing over Osama bin Laden to a neutral country in return for a bombing halt, but only if the Taliban were given evidence of bin Laden's involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> The U.S. rejected this offer, and continued military operations. <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-i-Sharif</a> fell to United Front troops of <a href="/wiki/Ustad_Atta_Mohammad_Noor" class="mw-redirect" title="Ustad Atta Mohammad Noor">Ustad Atta Mohammad Noor</a> and Abdul Rashid Dostum on November 9, triggering a cascade of provinces falling with minimal resistance.</p>
