<p>Pakistan, at least up to 2011, has always strongly denied all links with Taliban.<sup id="cite_ref-Pakdenies_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pakdenies-302">[302]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pak-US_alliance_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pak-US_alliance-303">[303]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-US-Pak_strained_alliance_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US-Pak_strained_alliance-304">[304]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 15, 2014 Pakistan army launches operation ‘Zarb-e-Azb’ in North Waziristan to remove and root-out Taliban from Pakistan. In this operation 327 hardcore terrorists had been killed while 45 hideouts and 2 bomb making factories of terrorists were destroyed in North Waziristan Agency as the operation continues.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Tehrik-i-Taliban_Pakistan_(Pakistani_Taliban)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tehrik-i-Taliban_Pakistan_.28Pakistani_Taliban.29">Tehrik-i-Taliban Pakistan (Pakistani Taliban)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Waziristan" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic Emirate of Waziristan">Islamic Emirate of Waziristan</a>, <a href="/wiki/War_in_North-West_Pakistan" title="War in North-West Pakistan">War in North-West Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wana" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Wana">Battle of Wana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tehrik-i-Taliban_Pakistan" title="Tehrik-i-Taliban Pakistan">Tehrik-i-Taliban Pakistan</a></div>
<p>Before the creation of the Tehrik-i-Taliban (Pakistan), some of their leaders and fighters were part of the 8,000 Pakistani militants fighting in the <a href="/wiki/Civil_war_in_Afghanistan_(1996-2001)" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil war in Afghanistan (1996-2001)">War in Afghanistan (1996–2001)</a> and the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001-present)" class="mw-redirect" title="War in Afghanistan (2001-present)">War in Afghanistan (2001-present)</a> against the <a href="/wiki/United_Islamic_Front" class="mw-redirect" title="United Islamic Front">United Islamic Front</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> forces.<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph-59">[59]</a></sup> Most of them hail from the Pakistani side of the Af-Pak border regions. After the fall of the Afghan Taliban in late 2001 most Pakistani militants including members of today's TTP fled home to Pakistan.</p>
<p>After the creation of the Tehrik-i-Taliban Pakistan in 2007, headed by <a href="/wiki/Baitullah_Mehsud" title="Baitullah Mehsud">Baitullah Mehsud</a>, its members have officially defined goals to establish their rule over Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Federally_Administered_Tribal_Areas" title="Federally Administered Tribal Areas">Federally Administered Tribal Areas</a>. They engage the Pakistani army in heavy combat operations. Some intelligence analysts believe that the TTP's attacks on the Pakistani government, police and army strained the TTP's relations with the Afghan Taliban.<sup id="cite_ref-Tighe-Katz_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tighe-Katz-311">[311]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-scott_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scott-312">[312]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sanctionsunc_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanctionsunc-313">[313]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Afghan Taliban and the Tehrik-i-Taliban Pakistan differ greatly in their history, leadership and goals although they share a common interpretation of Islam and are both predominantly Pashtun.<sup id="cite_ref-scott_312-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scott-312">[312]</a></sup> The Afghan Taliban have no affiliation with the Tehrik-i-Taliban Pakistan and routinely deny any connection to the TTP. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> quoted a spokesman for the Afghan Taliban stating that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We don't like to be involved with them, as we have rejected all affiliation with Pakistani Taliban fighters ... We have sympathy for them as Muslims, but beside that, there is nothing else between us.<sup id="cite_ref-gall2009327_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gall2009327-314">[314]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>It is alleged that Afghan Taliban relied on support by the Pakistani army in the past and are still supported by them today in their campaign to control Afghanistan. Regular Pakistani army troops fought alongside the Afghan Taliban in the <a href="/wiki/Civil_war_in_Afghanistan_(1996%E2%80%932001)" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil war in Afghanistan (1996–2001)">War in Afghanistan (1996–2001)</a>. Major leaders of the Afghan Taliban including <a href="/wiki/Mullah_Omar" class="mw-redirect" title="Mullah Omar">Mullah Omar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jalaluddin_Haqqani" title="Jalaluddin Haqqani">Jalaluddin Haqqani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siraj_Haqqani" class="mw-redirect" title="Siraj Haqqani">Siraj Haqqani</a> are believed to enjoy or have enjoyed safe haven in Pakistan. In 2006 <a href="/wiki/Jalaluddin_Haqqani" title="Jalaluddin Haqqani">Jalaluddin Haqqani</a> was allegedly called a 'Pakistani asset' by a senior official of Inter-Services Intelligence. Pakistan denies any links with Haqqani or other terrorist groups. Haqqani himself has denied any links with Pakistan as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_71-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_2008-09-09_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2008-09-09-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-George_Washington_University_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-George_Washington_University-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-315">[315]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-newser1_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newser1-316">[316]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-armytimes1_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armytimes1-317">[317]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-windsorstar1_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-windsorstar1-318">[318]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tribune1_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tribune1-319">[319]</a></sup></p>
<p>Afghan Taliban leader <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Omar" title="Mohammed Omar">Mullah Omar</a> asked the Tehrik-i-Taliban Pakistan in late 2008 and early 2009 to stop attacks inside Pakistan, to change their focus as an organization and to fight the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Army" title="Afghan National Army">Afghan National Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/ISAF" class="mw-redirect" title="ISAF">ISAF</a> forces in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> instead. In late December 2008 and early January 2009 he sent a delegation, led by former <a href="/wiki/Guantanamo_Bay_detention_camp" title="Guantanamo Bay detention camp">Guantanamo Bay detainee</a> <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Ghulam_Rasoul" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdullah Ghulam Rasoul">Mullah Abdullah Zakir</a>, to persuade leading members of the TTP to put aside differences with Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-gall2009327_314-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gall2009327-314">[314]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some regional experts state the common name "Taliban" may be more misleading than illuminating.<sup id="cite_ref-scott_312-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scott-312">[312]</a></sup> Gilles Dorronsoro, a scholar of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> currently at the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Endowment_for_International_Peace" title="Carnegie Endowment for International Peace">Carnegie Endowment for International Peace</a> in Washington says:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The fact that they have the same name causes all kinds of confusion.<sup id="cite_ref-scott_312-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scott-312">[312]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>As the Pakistani Army began offensives against the Pakistani Taliban, many unfamiliar with the region thought incorrectly that the assault was against the Afghan Taliban of Mullah Omar which was not the case.<sup id="cite_ref-scott_312-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scott-312">[312]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Pakistani Taliban were put under sanctions by <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">U.N. Security Council</a> for terrorists attacks in Pakistan and the <a href="/wiki/2010_Times_Square_car_bombing_attempt" title="2010 Times Square car bombing attempt">2010 Times Square car bombing attempt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sanctionsunc_313-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanctionsunc-313">[313]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malakand_Taliban">Malakand Taliban</span></h3>
<p>Malakand Taliban is a militant outfit led by Sufi Muhammad and his son in law Molvi Fazalullah. Sufi Muhammad is in Pakistani government custody, however, Molvi Fazalullah is believed to be in Afghanistan. In the last week of May 2011, eight security personnel and civilians fell victim to four hundred armed Taliban who attacked Shaltalo check post in Dir, a frontier District of Khyber Pakhtunkhwa, located few kilometers away from Afghan border. Although, they have been linked with Waziristan-based Tehreek-e-Taliban Pakistan (TTP), the connection between these two groups was of symbolic nature.<sup id="cite_ref-bare_url_a_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bare_url_a-320">[320]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamid_Mir_interviewing_Osama_bin_Laden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Hamid_Mir_interviewing_Osama_bin_Laden.jpg/220px-Hamid_Mir_interviewing_Osama_bin_Laden.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Hamid_Mir_interviewing_Osama_bin_Laden.jpg/330px-Hamid_Mir_interviewing_Osama_bin_Laden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Hamid_Mir_interviewing_Osama_bin_Laden.jpg/440px-Hamid_Mir_interviewing_Osama_bin_Laden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="451" data-file-height="292" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamid_Mir_interviewing_Osama_bin_Laden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pakistani journalist <a href="/wiki/Hamid_Mir" title="Hamid Mir">Hamid Mir</a> interviewing <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a> in Afghanistan, November 2001.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1996, bin Laden moved to Afghanistan from <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>. He came without invitation, and sometimes irritated Mullah Omar with his declaration of war and fatwas against citizens of third-party countries, but relations between the two groups improved over time, to the point that Mullah Omar rebuffed his group's patron Saudi Arabia, insulting Saudi minister <a href="/wiki/Turki_bin_Faisal_Al_Saud" title="Turki bin Faisal Al Saud">Prince Turki</a> while reneging on an earlier promise to turn bin Laden over to the Saudis.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bin Laden was able to forge an <a href="/wiki/Military_alliance" title="Military alliance">alliance</a> between the Taliban and <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a>. The al-Qaeda-trained <a href="/wiki/055_Brigade" title="055 Brigade">055 Brigade</a> integrated with the Taliban army between 1997 and 2001. Several hundred Arab and Afghan fighters sent by bin Laden assisted the Taliban in the <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif#1997.E2.80.931998_civil_unrest" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-e-Sharif slaughter in 1998</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup> The so-called Brigade 055 was also responsible for massacres against civilians in other parts of Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph-59">[59]</a></sup> From 1996 to 2001, the organization of Osama Bin Laden and Ayman al-Zawahiri had become a virtual state within the Taliban state.</p>
<p>Taliban-al-Qaeda connections were also strengthened by the reported marriage of one of bin Laden's sons to Omar's daughter. While in Afghanistan, bin Laden may have helped finance the Taliban.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/1998_United_States_embassy_bombings" title="1998 United States embassy bombings">1998 U.S. embassy bombings</a> in Africa, <a href="/wiki/FBI_Most_Wanted_Terrorists#Osama_bin_Laden" title="FBI Most Wanted Terrorists">bin Laden</a> and several al-Qaeda members were indicted in U.S. criminal court. The Taliban rejected <a href="/wiki/Extradition" title="Extradition">extradition</a> requests by the U.S., variously claiming that bin Laden had "gone missing", or that Washington "cannot provide any evidence or any proof" that bin Laden is involved in terrorist activities and that "without any evidence, bin Laden is a man without sin... he is a free man." <sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">[326]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CBS21Oct01_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBS21Oct01-328">[328]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taliban_insurgency_in_Afghanistan_(2015%E2%80%93present).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Taliban_insurgency_in_Afghanistan_%282015%E2%80%93present%29.svg/220px-Taliban_insurgency_in_Afghanistan_%282015%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Taliban_insurgency_in_Afghanistan_%282015%E2%80%93present%29.svg/330px-Taliban_insurgency_in_Afghanistan_%282015%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Taliban_insurgency_in_Afghanistan_%282015%E2%80%93present%29.svg/440px-Taliban_insurgency_in_Afghanistan_%282015%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1807" data-file-height="1331" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taliban_insurgency_in_Afghanistan_(2015%E2%80%93present).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Current military situation, as of 27 February 2016.
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ebc0b3; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Under control of the <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Afghanistan" title="Politics of Afghanistan">Afghan Government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Resolute_Support_Mission" title="Resolute Support Mission">NATO</a>, and <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2015%E2%80%93present)#Allied_militias" class="mw-redirect" title="War in Afghanistan (2015–present)">Allies</a></div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffffff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Under control of the Taliban, <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Jihad_Union" title="Islamic Jihad Union">Allies</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Evidence against bin Laden included courtroom testimony and <a href="/wiki/Satellite_phone" title="Satellite phone">satellite phone</a> records. Bin Laden in turn, praised the Taliban as the "only Islamic government" in existence, and lauded Mullah Omar for his destruction of idols such as the <a href="/wiki/Buddhas_of_Bamyan" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhas of Bamyan">Buddhas of Bamyan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">[331]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the end of 2008, the Taliban was in talks to sever all ties with al-Qaeda.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">[333]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, Alex Strick van Linschoten and Felix Kuehn at New York University's Center on International Cooperation claimed that the two groups did not get along at times before the September 11 attacks, and they have continued to fight since on account of their differences.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">[334]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2012, an anonymous senior-ranking Taliban commander stated that "Our people consider al-Qaeda to be a plague that was sent down to us by the heavens. Some even concluded that al-Qaeda are actually the spies of America. Originally, the Taliban were naive and ignorant of politics and welcomed al-Qaeda into their homes. But al-Qaeda abused our hospitality." He went on to further claim that about 70% of the Taliban are angry with al-Qaeda, revealing the icy relationship between the two groups.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">[335]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">[336]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span></h3>
<p>Iran has historically been an enemy of the Taliban. In early August 1998, after attacking the city of <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar</a>, Taliban forces killed several thousand civilians and 10 Iranian diplomats and intelligence officers in the Iranian consulate. Alleged radio intercepts indicate Mullah Omar personally approved the killings.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">[337]</a></sup> In the following crisis between Iran and the Taliban, the Iranian government amassed up to 200,000 regular troops on the Afghan-Iranian border.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">[338]</a></sup> War was eventually averted.</p>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/United_States_armed_forces" class="mw-redirect" title="United States armed forces">U.S. senior military officials</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gates" title="Robert Gates">Robert Gates</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">[339]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stanley_A._McChrystal" title="Stanley A. McChrystal">Stanley McChrystal</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">[340]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Petraeus" title="David Petraeus">David Petraeus</a><sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">[341]</a></sup> and others believe that Iran's <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Guardians_of_the_Islamic_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Army of the Guardians of the Islamic Revolution">Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps</a> nowadays is involved in helping the Taliban to a certain extent. Reports in which <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> states accused Iran of supplying and training some Taliban insurgents started coming forward since 2004/2005.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"We did interdict a shipment, without question the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Guardians_of_the_Islamic_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Army of the Guardians of the Islamic Revolution">Revolutionary Guard</a>'s core <a href="/wiki/Quds_Force" title="Quds Force">Quds Force</a>, through a known Taliban facilitator. Three of the individuals were killed... 48 122 millimetre rockets were intercepted with their various components... Iranians certainly view as making life more difficult for us if Afghanistan is unstable. We don't have that kind of relationship with the Iranians. That's why I am particularly troubled by the interception of weapons coming from Iran. But we know that it's more than weapons; it's money; it's also according to some reports, training at Iranian camps as well."<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">[342]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/General_(United_States)" title="General (United States)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Petraeus" title="David Petraeus">David Petraeus</a>, Commander of US-NATO forces in Afghanistan, March 16, 2011</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>
<p>The United States never recognized the Taliban government in Afghanistan. However, Ahmed Rashid states that the U.S. indirectly supported the Taliban through its ally in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> between 1994 and 1996 because Washington viewed the Taliban as anti-<a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="Iran–United States relations">Iranian</a>, anti-<a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a> and pro-Western. Washington furthermore hoped that the Taliban would support development planned by the U.S.-based oil company <i><a href="/wiki/Unocal" class="mw-redirect" title="Unocal">Unocal</a></i>. For example, it made no comment when the Taliban captured Herat in 1995, and expelled thousands of girls from schools. In late 1997, American Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_Albright" title="Madeleine Albright">Madeleine Albright</a> began to distance the U.S. from the Taliban, and the American-based oil company <a href="/wiki/Unocal" class="mw-redirect" title="Unocal">Unocal</a> withdrew from negotiations on pipeline construction from Central Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">[343]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">[344]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">[345]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">[346]</a></sup></p>
<p>One day before the August 1998 capture of Mazar, bin Laden affiliates <a href="/wiki/1998_United_States_embassy_bombings" title="1998 United States embassy bombings">bombed two U.S. embassies</a> in Africa, killing 224 and wounding 4,500, mostly Africans. The U.S. responded by launching cruise missiles on suspected terrorist camps in Afghanistan, killing over 20 though failing to kill bin Laden or even many Al-Qaeda. Mullah Omar condemned the missile attack and American President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>. Saudi Arabia expelled the Taliban envoy in protest over the refusal to turn over bin Laden, and after Mullah Omar allegedly insulted the Saudi royal family. In mid-October the U.N. <a href="/wiki/Security_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Security Council">Security Council</a> voted unanimously to ban commercial aircraft flights to and from Afghanistan, and freeze its bank accounts worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">[347]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">[348]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">[349]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GIs_burn_a_house_described_as_a_Taliban_safehouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/GIs_burn_a_house_described_as_a_Taliban_safehouse.jpg/170px-GIs_burn_a_house_described_as_a_Taliban_safehouse.jpg" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/GIs_burn_a_house_described_as_a_Taliban_safehouse.jpg/255px-GIs_burn_a_house_described_as_a_Taliban_safehouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/GIs_burn_a_house_described_as_a_Taliban_safehouse.jpg/340px-GIs_burn_a_house_described_as_a_Taliban_safehouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1424" data-file-height="2144" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GIs_burn_a_house_described_as_a_Taliban_safehouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US soldiers burn a suspected Taliban safehouse.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adjusting its <a href="/wiki/Counterinsurgency" class="mw-redirect" title="Counterinsurgency">counterinsurgency</a> strategy, in October 2009, the U.S announced plans to pay Taliban fighters to switch sides.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">[350]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 26, 2009, in an interview with <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Christiane_Amanpour" title="Christiane Amanpour">Christiane Amanpour</a>, President <a href="/wiki/Hamid_Karzai" title="Hamid Karzai">Hamid Karzai</a> said there is an "urgent need" for negotiations with the Taliban, and made it clear that the Obama administration had opposed such talks. There was no formal American response.<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">[351]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">[352]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2009, Asian Times Online reported that the Taliban had offered to give the U.S. "legal guarantees" that they would not allow Afghanistan to be used for attacks on other countries, and that there had been no formal American response.<sup id="cite_ref-atimes.com_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atimes.com-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 6, U.S. officials indicated that they have not ruled out talks with the Taliban. Several days later it was reported that Gates saw potential for reconciliation with the Taliban, but not with Al-Qaeda. Furthermore, he said that reconciliation would politically end the insurgency and the war. But he said reconciliation must be on the Afghan government's terms, and that the Taliban must be subject to the sovereignty of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">[353]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">[354]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, General McChrystal said his troop surge could lead to a negotiated peace with the Taliban.<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">[355]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span></h3>
<p>After the 9/11 attacks, the United Kingdom froze the Taliban's assets in the U.K., nearly $200 million by early October 2001. The U.K. also supported the U.S. decision to remove the Taliban, both politically and militarily.<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">[356]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">[357]</a></sup></p>
<p>The UN agreed that NATO would act on its behalf, focusing on counter-terrorist operations in Afghanistan after the Taliban had been "defeated". The United Kingdom took operational responsibility for <a href="/wiki/Helmand_Province" title="Helmand Province">Helmand Province</a>, a major poppy-growing province in southern Afghanistan, deploying troops there in the summer of 2006, and encountered resistance by re-formed Taliban forces allegedly entering Afghanistan from Pakistan. The Taliban turned towards the use of <a href="/wiki/Improvised_explosive_devices" class="mw-redirect" title="Improvised explosive devices">improvised explosive devices</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">[358]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 2008 the United Kingdom announced plans to pay Taliban fighters to switch sides or lay down arms; the proceeding year the U.K. government supported negotiations with the Taliban.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">[359]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">[360]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span></h3>
<p>India is one of the Taliban's most outspoken critics. India did not recognize the Taliban regime in Afghanistan and instead maintained close strategic and military ties with the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Alliance" title="Northern Alliance">Northern Alliance</a> so as to contain the rise of Taliban during the 1990s. India was one of the closest allies of former Afghan president <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Najibullah" title="Mohammad Najibullah">Mohammad Najibullah</a> and strongly condemned his public execution by the Taliban. Pakistan and Kashmir-based militant groups thought to have ties with the Taliban have historically been involved in the <a href="/wiki/Kashmir_insurgency" class="mw-redirect" title="Kashmir insurgency">Kashmir insurgency</a> targeted against Indian security forces.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">[361]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">[362]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pigott_54_363-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigott_54-363">[363]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">[364]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 1999, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Airlines_Flight_814" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Airlines Flight 814">Indian Airlines Flight 814</a> en route from <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a> to <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a> was hijacked and taken to <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>. The Taliban moved its militias near the hijacked aircraft, supposedly to prevent <a href="/wiki/Special_Forces_of_India" title="Special Forces of India">Indian special forces</a> from storming the aircraft, and stalled the negotiations between India and the hijackers for days. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> later reported that there were credible links between the hijackers and the Taliban. As a part of the deal to free the plane, India released three militants. The Taliban gave a safe passage to the hijackers and the released militants.<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">[365]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">[366]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the hijacking, India drastically increased its efforts to help Massoud, providing an arms depot in <a href="/wiki/Dushanbe" title="Dushanbe">Dushanbe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>. India also provided a wide range of high-altitude warfare equipment, helicopter technicians, medical services, and tactical advice. According to one report, Indian military support to anti-Taliban forces totaled US$70 million, including five <a href="/wiki/Mil_Mi-17" title="Mil Mi-17">Mil Mi-17</a> helicopters, and US$8 million worth of high-altitude equipment in 2001. India extensively supported the new administration in Afghanistan, leading several reconstruction projects and by 2001 had emerged as the country's largest regional donor.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">[367]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">[368]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">[369]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">[370]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">[371]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">[372]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the wake of recent <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_in_India" title="Terrorism in India">terrorist attacks in India</a>, there have been growing concerns about fundamentalist organisations such as the Taliban seeking to expand their activities into India. During the <a href="/wiki/2011_ICC_Cricket_World_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 ICC Cricket World Cup">2011 ICC Cricket World Cup</a> which was co-hosted in India, Pakistani Interior Minister <a href="/wiki/Rehman_Malik" title="Rehman Malik">Rehman Malik</a> and Interpol chief <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Noble" title="Ronald Noble">Ronald Noble</a> revealed that a terrorist bid to disrupt the tournament had been foiled; following a conference with Noble, Malik said that the Taliban had begun to base their activities in India with reports from neighboring countries exposing their activities in the country and a Sri Lankan terrorist planning to target cricketers was arrested in Colombo. In 2009, the <i><a href="/wiki/Times_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Times of India">Times of India</a></i> called for India to reassess its Taliban threat.<sup id="cite_ref-373" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-373">[373]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-374" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-374">[374]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">[375]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-376" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-376">[376]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Nations_and_NGOs">United Nations and NGOs</span></h3>
<p>Despite the aid of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (UN) and <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organizations</a> (NGOs) given (see <a href="/wiki/Taliban#Afghanistan_during_Taliban_rule" title="Taliban">§ Afghanistan during Taliban rule</a>), the Taliban's attitude in 1996-2001 toward the UN and NGOs was often one of suspicion. The UN did not recognize the Taliban as the legitimate government of Afghanistan, most foreign donors and aid workers were non-Muslims, and the Taliban vented fundamental objections to the sort of 'help' the UN offered. As the Taliban's Attorney General Maulvi Jalil-ullah Maulvizada put it in 1997:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Let us state what sort of education the UN wants. This is a big infidel policy which gives such obscene freedom to women which would lead to <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a> and herald the destruction of Islam. In any Islamic country where adultery becomes common, that country is destroyed and enters the domination of the infidels because their men become like women and women cannot defend themselves. Anyone who talks to us should do so within Islam's framework. The Holy Koran cannot adjust itself to other people's requirements, people should adjust themselves to the requirements of the Holy Koran.<sup id="cite_ref-377" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-377">[377]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In July 1998, the Taliban closed "all NGO offices" by force after those organizations refused to move to a bombed-out former <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_technology" title="Institute of technology">Polytechnic</a> College as ordered.<sup id="cite_ref-bmj_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmj-96">[96]</a></sup> One month later the UN offices were also shut down.<sup id="cite_ref-rashid.2C71_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rashid.2C71-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Around 2000, the UN drew up sanctions against officials and leaders of Taliban, because of their harbouring <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a>. Several of them Taliban leaders have subsequently been killed.<sup id="cite_ref-telegraphJan2010_378-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telegraphJan2010-378">[378]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, British foreign secretary Miliband and US Secretary Hillary Clinton had called for talks with 'regular Taliban fighters' while bypassing their top leaders who supposedly were 'committed to global jihad'. Kai Eide, the top UN official in Afghanistan, however called for talks with Taliban at the highest level, suggesting <a href="/wiki/Mullah_Omar" class="mw-redirect" title="Mullah Omar">Mullah Omar</a>-- even though Omar had recently dismissed such overtures as long as foreign troops were in Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">[379]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, the U.N lifted sanctions on the Taliban, and requested that Taliban leaders and others be removed from terrorism watch lists. In 2010 the U.S. and Europe announced support for President Karzai's latest attempt to negotiate peace with the Taliban.<sup id="cite_ref-telegraphJan2010_378-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telegraphJan2010-378">[378]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-380" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-380">[380]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-381" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-381">[381]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-Giraldo-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Giraldo_55-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Giraldo_55-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Giraldo, Jeanne K. (2007). <i>Terrorism Financing and State Responses: A Comparative Perspective</i>. Stanford University Press. p.&#160;96. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8047-5566-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8047-5566-5">978-0-8047-5566-5</a>. <q>Pakistan provided military support, including arms, ammunition, fuel, and military advisers, to the Taliban through its Directorate for Inter-Services Intelligence (ISI)</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATaliban&amp;rft.aufirst=Jeanne+K.&amp;rft.aulast=Giraldo&amp;rft.btitle=Terrorism+Financing+and+State+Responses%3A+A+Comparative+Perspective&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8047-5566-5&amp;rft.pages=96&amp;rft.pub=Stanford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch-PST-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch-PST_56-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch-PST_56-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.hrw.org/reports/2001/afghan2/Afghan0701-02.htm">"Pakistan's support of the Taliban"</a>. Human Rights Watch. 2000. <q>Of all the foreign powers involved in efforts to sustain and manipulate the ongoing fighting [in Afghanistan], Pakistan is distinguished both by the sweep of its objectives and the scale of its efforts, which include soliciting funding for the Taliban, bankrolling Taliban operations, providing diplomatic support as the Taliban's virtual emissaries abroad, arranging training for Taliban fighters, recruiting skilled and unskilled manpower to serve in Taliban armies, planning and directing offensives, providing and facilitating shipments of ammunition and fuel, and ... directly providing combat support.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATaliban&amp;rft.atitle=Pakistan%27s+support+of+the+Taliban&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.hrw.org%2Freports%2F2001%2Fafghan2%2FAfghan0701-02.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-longwarjournal1-quote-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-longwarjournal1-quote_57-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Joscelyn, Thomas (2011-09-22). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.longwarjournal.org/archives/2011/09/admiral_mullen_pakis.php">"Admiral Mullen: Pakistani ISI sponsoring Haqqani attacks"</a>. <i>The Long War Journal</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2011-12-01</span></span>. <q>During a Senate Armed Services Committee hearing today, Admiral Michael Mullen, the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, highlighted the Pakistani Inter-Services Intelligence Agency's role in sponsoring the Haqqani Network – including attacks on American forces in Afghanistan. "The fact remains that the Quetta Shura [Taliban] and the Haqqani Network operate from Pakistan with impunity," Mullen said in his written testimony. "Extremist organizations serving as proxies of the government of Pakistan are attacking Afghan troops and civilians as well as US soldiers." Mullen continued: "For example, we believe the Haqqani Network—which has long enjoyed the support and protection of the Pakistani government and is, in many ways, a strategic arm of Pakistan's Inter-Services Intelligence Agency—is responsible for the September 13th attacks against the U.S. Embassy in Kabul."</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATaliban&amp;rft.atitle=Admiral+Mullen%3A+Pakistani+ISI+sponsoring+Haqqani+attacks&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas&amp;rft.aulast=Joscelyn&amp;rft.date=2011-09-22&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Long+War+Journal&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.longwarjournal.org%2Farchives%2F2011%2F09%2Fadmiral_mullen_pakis.php&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
